According to the Women's Labor Law, an employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee for 60 days after the end of the period of childbirth and parenthood, except with special permission from the Minister of Industry, Trade and Employment. The Minister will not authorize dismissal unless he is satisfied that the dismissal is not related to childbirth or the period of childbirth and parenthood, and that the employer's business has ceased to operate or the employer has been declared bankrupt. After this 60-day period, there is no special restriction on the dismissal of the employee in terms of this law.
No, it is prohibited to fire an employee who has been drafted into the reserves without special permission. The law provides protection against firing for employees serving in the reserves:1. It is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or anticipated service in the reserves.2. It is prohibited to fire an employee during his reserve service.3. Regarding reserve service of more than two consecutive days, the employee may not be fired even for 30 days after the end of the reserve service, without special permission.4. In the case of reserve service exceeding 60 days, the protection against firing continues for 60 days after the end of the service. Firings in violation of this law will be void, and the employer may be subject to fines.
Yes, the employer is obligated to set aside vacation days during reserve service. According to the Annual Vacation Law, 5711-1951, Section 5(a)(1), the days the employee served in reserve service will not be included in the number of vacation days. In other words, reserve days are not considered vacation days, and the employee is entitled to accrue vacation days even during the reserve service period. In addition, Section 41A(a) of the Discharged Soldiers Law states that an employee's absence from work due to reserve service will not be considered a break in the continuity of his work at the enterprise, which ensures the continued accrual of rights, including vacation days, during reserve service.

 According to the law, the decision to work remotely is part of the employer's administrative right. However, the employer must consider the employee's request to work remotely in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner given the existing circumstances. Therefore, while employees can request to work remotely, they do not have an absolute right to demand it, and this depends on the employer's discretion.
According to the law, an employer is prohibited from dismissing a pregnant employee who has worked for at least 6 months for that employer, without obtaining a special permit from the Commissioner of the Women's Employment Law at the Ministry of Labor. If dismissals were made in violation of this law, the dismissals are null and void. In the case of illegal dismissal during pregnancy, the employee is entitled to return to her job. If the employer refuses to reinstate her, she can apply to the Labor Court. It is important to note that the law protects pregnant employees from dismissal, but there are exceptional cases in which a permit for dismissal can be obtained, for example if the business closes or in the case of serious disciplinary violations.
According to the law, there is no prohibition on firing an employee who is absent from work due to alarms, unless a specific order has been issued by the Home Front Command or the government preventing the employee from coming to work. Therefore, in general, there is an obligation to report to work even when there are alarms, unless another order has been issued. Regarding working from home, the decision is part of the employer's administrative right. However, the employer must consider the employee's request to work from home in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner, taking into account the existing circumstances. It is recommended to discuss this issue with the employer and reach a mutual agreement.
According to the law, the decision regarding remote work is part of the employer's administrative right. However, the employer must consider in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner the employee's request to work from home in light of the existing circumstances, such as in the case of alarms. If there are no restrictions on behalf of the employer, it is possible to request to work remotely. It is important to note that if there are no restrictions, there is an obligation to report to work.
According to the law, you are not required to report for work service in an essential enterprise. Since your child is six months old, you are in this period and are therefore exempt from reporting for work service despite the order issued to you.

According to the law, it is prohibited to violate the rights of an employee due to reserve service: 1. An employee's absence from work due to reserve service shall not be considered a break in the continuity of his work. 2. An employer may not dismiss an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to service or anticipated service, including due to the frequency or duration of the service. Such dismissals are void. 3. It is prohibited to dismiss an employee during reserve service and for 30 days after its completion (if the service was more than two days), or to harm the scope of his job or income, without permission from the Employment Committee. 4. In the event of a claim for dismissal due to reserve service, the employer must prove that the dismissal was for another reason. 5. This protection also applies to the non-renewal of a fixed-term employment contract under certain conditions. This law protects the rights of employees serving in the reserves and prevents them from being harmed due to their service.
 According to the law, an employer is prohibited from firing an employee during a certain period after reserve service without special permission. If your reserve service was more than two consecutive days, firing is prohibited for 30 days after the end of the service. If you served more than 60 days, the prohibition applies for 60 days after the end of the reserve. If you fired during this period without permission from the Employment Committee, the firing may be invalid. It is recommended to contact the employer and explain the legal situation. If the employer refuses to cancel the firing, you can contact the Employment Committee or the Labor Court for legal assistance.

According to the law, as a reserve employee, you have a number of rights: 1. Your absence from work due to reserve service will not be considered a break in the continuity of your work. 2. Your employer may not fire you due to your reserve service, your call to reserve service, or anticipated reserve service. If you are fired for these reasons, the dismissal is void. 3. Your employer may not fire you during your reserve service and for 30 days after the end of your service (if the service was more than two consecutive days) without special permission from the Employment Committee. 4. The period of reserve service will not be included in the number of days of advance notice for dismissal. 5. If you have a child under the age of 14 (or up to the age of 21 with special needs), there are additional protections for your spouse from being fired or taken on unpaid leave during your reserve service. It is important to note that these rights are protected by law, and if you feel that your rights have been violated, you have the option of contacting the Employment Committee or the appropriate authorities.
 The Employment Committee is a committee appointed by the Minister of Defense for the purposes of the Discharged Soldiers (Return to Work) Law. The committee consists of four members: 1. Chairman - a person qualified to serve as a Magistrate's Court judge, appointed by the Minister of Justice for a single period of six years. 2. The committee's work center - an employee of the Bureau for the Guidance of Discharged Soldiers in the Ministry of Defense. 3. A representative of the largest labor organization in the country. 4. A representative of representative employer organizations in the country. The members of the committee (except the chairman) are appointed for a period of three years, and may be reappointed for additional periods. Notice of the appointment of an Employment Committee is published in Reshumot.
According to the information provided, there is no specific reference to the rights of victims of hostilities in the Discharged Soldiers Law. However, it is mentioned that eligibility for injury benefits must be checked with the National Insurance Institute for absence from work due to injury in a terrorist act. It is recommended to contact the National Insurance Institute to clarify the full rights to which victims of hostilities are entitled. According to the Law for the Protection of Workers in Times of Emergency, there is no direct reference to the obligation to pay wages to protected workers. The law focuses mainly on prohibiting dismissals and maintaining work continuity, but does not explicitly specify the obligation to pay wages. However, the law allows an employee or an employee organization to file a lawsuit for violating the provisions of the law. In the event of a dispute regarding the payment of wages, a lawsuit can be filed with the Labor Court. It is important to note that the decision regarding the payment of wages in the event of the closure of a business due to the situation is subject to the employer's decision, and in certain cases the employer may be entitled to compensation for the payment of wages to employees who were absent from work due to the situation.
 No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (Reserve Order) without special permission. According to the law, it is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or his expected service in reserve service. If the employee serves in the reserves for more than two consecutive days, he may not be fired during his service and for 30 days after the end of his reserve service, except with special permission from the Employment Committee. In the case of reserve service for more than 60 days, the period of protection from dismissal is extended to 60 days after the end of the service. If dismissals were made in violation of these provisions, the dismissals are void.
According to the law, it is prohibited to dismiss an employee who has been recognized as a victim of hostilities. Section 2(b1) of the Emergency Workers Protection Law states that an employer shall not dismiss an employee for absence from work or failure to perform work during the temporary order period if the employee is kidnapped or missing as a result of hostilities or war. The law protects these employees from dismissal, and if an employee is dismissed in violation of these provisions, the dismissal is void.

According to the law, it is prohibited to require someone to cease reserve service as a condition of employment. The law prohibits firing or harming an employee because of his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or his expected service in reserve service. A requirement to cease reserve service as a condition of employment will be considered a violation of the employee's rights and is prohibited by law.

Under Israeli law, the decision to work remotely is part of the employer’s administrative right. However, the employer must consider the employee’s request to work from home in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner given the prevailing circumstances. Therefore, while employees can request to work remotely, they do not have an absolute right to demand it, and this depends on the employer’s decision and the specific circumstances.

The law grants a number of rights and protections to spouses of reserve service members: 1. Protection against dismissal: An employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee due to his/her spouse's reserve service or due to the employee's absence related to it. 2. Shortening the workday: An employee is entitled to shorten the workday by one hour when the spouse is serving in the reserves for at least 5 consecutive days, provided that they have a child up to the age of 13 and they work full-time. 3. Paid days of absence: Spouses are entitled to paid days of absence according to the number of days of cumulative reserve service of the spouse, starting from 2 days for 31-60 days of reserve service and up to 8 days for 121 days of reserve service or more. 4. Protection against taking unpaid leave: In certain cases, such as prolonged reserve service or in emergency circumstances, a special permit is required to take the employee on unpaid leave.
According to the law, the calculation of the sick leave period depends on the type of employee: 1. For a salaried employee - the sick leave period includes all days of his illness, including weekly rest days and holidays. 2. For a salaried employee who worked full-time - the sick leave period includes all days of his illness, excluding weekly rest days and holidays. Since your job is defined as Sunday through Thursday, it seems that you fall into the second category. Therefore, according to the law, the employer should not deduct sick leave from you on Friday and Saturday, which are your weekly rest days. However, it is important to note that the law allows the Minister of Labor to establish additional regulations on the subject. Therefore, it is recommended to check your employment contract and the company's policy regarding sick leave to ensure that there are no special agreements on this issue.
No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (reserve call-up order) without special permission. According to the law, it is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call-up to reserve service, or his expected service in the reserves. If the employee is fired in violation of these provisions, the firing is void. In addition, there is protection against firing during and after reserve service: 1. For reserve service of more than two consecutive days and less than 60 days: The employee may not be fired during the service and for 30 days after its completion, without special permission. 2. For reserve service of 60 days or more: The employee may not be fired during the service and for 60 days after its completion, without special permission. It is important to note that absence due to reserve service is not considered a break in the employee's continuous work.
 According to the law, the decision to work remotely is part of the employer's administrative right. However, the employer must consider the employee's request to work remotely in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner given the existing circumstances. Therefore, while employees can request to work remotely, they do not have an absolute right to demand it, and this depends on the employer's discretion.
According to the law, when an employee returns to work from maternity leave, she is entitled to maintain her rights dependent on seniority with the employer. Section 6(t) of the Women's Labor Law states that "absence from work due to a period of maternity and parenthood according to the provisions of this section shall not affect the rights dependent on seniority with the employer." In other words, the period of maternity leave is considered for the purpose of accumulating seniority and rights deriving from it, such as convalescence pay, sick days and severance pay. However, it is important to note that the accumulation of annual leave days is not included in these rights.
According to the current information, an employee who continues to work concurrently with reserve service (Order 8) is entitled to receive his regular salary for the hours of work actually performed, in addition to the reserve compensation. The daily salary will be calculated based on the actual hours worked at the regular workplace. It should be noted that the employee is also entitled to receive the reserve compensation from the National Insurance Institute for the days of service. For additional details regarding the calculation of reserve compensation, it is recommended to contact the National Insurance Institute.
According to the law, after maternity and parental leave, you have the following rights upon returning to work: 1. The employer must allow you to return to the same position you held before the leave. 2. The period of maternity and parental leave is considered a period of work for the purposes of rights dependent on seniority with the employer. 3. If you have notified your desire to shorten your maternity and parental leave, the employer cannot postpone your return to work for more than three weeks from the date of notification. 4. You have the option of being absent from work for an additional period after maternity and parental leave, in accordance with the conditions specified in the law (such as the number of previous months of work). It is important to note that if you have not exercised all of these rights, you must return to work at the end of your maternity and parental leave.
 According to the Women's Employment Law, an employee is entitled to parental leave (also known as breastfeeding leave) from the end of the birth and parenthood period until the end of four months from that day. This is one hour a day, provided that the employee is employed full-time or in a position that covers at least 174 hours a month. Parental leave is in addition to regular breaks and is not deducted from wages. It is important to note that an employee (spouse) may also exercise the right to parental leave, alone or alternately with his or her spouse, provided that certain conditions specified in the law are met.

According to the Sick Pay (Absence Due to Child Illness) Law, 5753-1993, an employee who has a child under the age of 16 (including age 14) is entitled to credit days of absence due to his child's illness against his accumulated sick days, in the following number and under the following conditions: 1. If the spouse works and is not absent from work due to the child's illness - up to 8 days per year. 2. If the child is in the sole custody of the employee, or the employee is an independent parent - up to 16 days per year. It is important to note that this law adds to the employee's rights by law or agreement and does not detract from them.
According to the Emergency Workers Protection Law, if you have a child under the age of 14, you have the following rights in the workplace: 1. Your employer may not fire you for being absent from work to care for your child, if the educational institution where the child is studying or staying has been closed due to security orders. 2. This right applies provided that the child is in your sole custody or you are a single parent, or that your spouse is employed and not absent from work to care for the child. 3. If there is an appropriate arrangement for caring for the child at your or your spouse's workplace, the right to be absent from work will not apply. 4. If you are an employee of a labor contractor, the law also protects you from temporary or permanent termination of employment in these circumstances. 5. If you are dismissed in violation of these orders, the dismissal is void.

 Israeli law provides certain protections against dismissal in special cases. For example, there are restrictions on dismissing an employee who is pregnant or after maternity leave. There is also a mandatory hearing before dismissal or a material change in the terms of employment. However, without additional information about your specific circumstances, it is not possible to determine with certainty whether you are protected against dismissal. According to the law, if you have evacuated from your place of residence located in a locality listed in the appendix to the law, you are not obligated to physically come to work following the evacuation. The law prohibits the employer from dismissing an employee for his absence from work or failure to perform work during the period of the temporary order due to his evacuation from his place of residence located in a locality listed in the appendix. However, it is important to note that the law does not explicitly refer to the obligation to come to work, but only to protection against dismissal in the event of absence under these circumstances.

 Yes, following the evacuation you are protected from dismissal. According to the law, an employer may not dismiss an employee for absence from work or failure to perform work during the temporary order period if the employee has vacated his place of residence which is in the locality listed in the appendix to the law. In the event of dismissal in violation of this order, the dismissal will be void.
According to the law, there is generally an obligation to report to work unless one of the following cases applies: 1. An order was given during an attack or in a special situation on the home front that prevents the employee from coming to work. 2. The employee is required to look after his child due to the closure of an educational institution due to the state of emergency, and certain conditions are met (for example, the child is in his sole custody or the spouse works and cannot look after the child). 3. The employee is required to look after his child due to military service of the spouse or the child's other parent. If one of these cases does not apply, the employer may require the employee to physically come to the workplace.
According to the law, there is no specific reference to the situation of evacuating employees from their homes and the obligation to physically arrive at the workplace. However, the law protects employees from dismissal for absence from work or failure to perform work in circumstances of an emergency, when an official order has been issued preventing the employee from coming to work. In the case of an evacuation, if no official order has been issued preventing arrival at the workplace, the employer may still be entitled to require physical arrival. However, it is recommended to talk to the employer and try to reach an arrangement that takes into account the special situation. If the situation is unclear or if there is difficulty in reaching an agreement with the employer, it is recommended to seek individual legal advice or the relevant authorities at the Ministry of Labor for more precise instructions for the specific situation.

Yes, the employer has the option to require physical attendance at work. According to the Emergency Labor Service Law, a person called for labor service must report at the time and place specified in the order and perform the work assigned to him. The law states that the employee must "report at the time, place and before the authority specified in the order and perform any work assigned to him from time to time by the person specified in the order or on his behalf and continue regularly in this work as long as the order is in effect." Therefore, if the employer requires physical attendance at work as part of a labor service order, the employee is obligated to report.

If you were fired, there are several steps you should consider: 1. Check whether the dismissal was legal - for example, if you were in the reserves or in a period of protection after reservists, dismissal may be prohibited without special permission. 2. Make sure you received a due hearing before the dismissal - every employee has the right to a hearing before a final decision on dismissal. 3. If the dismissal was during the current state of emergency, check whether it was permitted by law - there are special protections for employees during this period. 4. If you believe that the dismissal was illegal, consider seeking legal advice or filing a complaint with the Labor Court. 5. In any case, make sure that you received all the rights you are entitled to when your employment is terminated. If you have additional questions or need more detailed information, it is recommended that you consult a lawyer specializing in labor law.

The condition set by your boss is illegal. According to the Demobilized Soldiers Law, an employer is prohibited from firing an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call-up to reserve service, or his expected service in reserve service. Also, after reserve service of over 60 days, the employer is obligated to return the employee to work and is prohibited from firing the employee for 60 days after the end of the reserves without special permission from the Employment Committee. Therefore, the requirement to commit not to return to the reserves in the next two years as a condition for continued employment is illegal. It is recommended that you explain to the employer that this requirement is illegal and that you are entitled to return to your job without conditions. If the employer complies with his demand, you can contact the Ministry of Labor or seek legal advice to further handle the matter.

By law, you are entitled to payment in this situation. As a single mother who was forced to stay at home due to the closure of educational institutions, you meet the criteria for receiving payment for the days of absence. The employer is obligated to pay you your regular salary (without supplements) up to a ceiling of 1,510 NIS per day, for the days you are absent from work due to the security situation. Payment is supposed to be made on the salary payment date in the month following the publication of the relevant extension order. It is important to note that if you took vacation days during this period, they are supposed to be returned to you.
According to the law, if you have received an order for work service in a babysitting service in an emergency settlement, you must report for work. Section 19 of the Emergency Work Service Law states that a person called for work service must report at the time and place specified in the order, and perform the work assigned to him. However, there are limitations on the amount of overtime permitted: 1. The court may award compensation, even if no financial damage was caused, and may also issue an injunction or a declaratory order if it deems that compensation alone would not be fair in the circumstances. It is important to note that the court will take into account the impact of its decision on labor relations in the workplace and on other employees.

According to the Emergency Workers Protection Law, an employee's absence from work due to a security situation is protected under certain circumstances: 1. An employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee for his absence from work or failure to perform work during the temporary order period, if the absence stems from the security situation. 2. Such absence will not be considered a break in the continuity of work and will not affect the employee's rights dependent on seniority. 3. This protection also applies to contractor employees. 4.  special area is defined by law as an area to which a declaration of a special situation in the home front applies. According to the law, when a special situation in the home front is declared, the Minister may declare by order that certain provisions will apply for the purpose of providing essential services to the population in that area or for the functioning of the entire economy. The special area may include specific localities, as detailed in the list in the appendix to the law, which includes localities in the Gaza Envelope and in the north of the country.

According to the Emergency Labor Service Law, an essential worker (or "internal recruit") is a worker in an essential enterprise or in a factory providing essential services who is called to work by order by the Minister. The main arrangements regarding an essential worker include: 1. The obligation to report to work in the essential enterprise in accordance with the call-up order. 2. Continuity of work and preservation of the employee's seniority rights. 3. The non-application of the protections against dismissal under the Emergency Labor Protection Law to employees called to work. 4. The possibility of accumulating unused vacation days for the next two years, without the need for the employer's approval, if the employee was employed in an essential enterprise. Yes, according to the law, the employee is entitled to absence from work in the event that there are no open learning frameworks for his children. Section 2(b)(1) of the Emergency Workers Protection Law states that an employer shall not dismiss an employee due to his absence from work to care for his child due to the closure of the educational institution where the child studies or resides, provided that one of the following applies: 1. The child is in the sole custody of the employee or the employee is an independent parent. 2. The employee's spouse is working and is not absent from work to care for the child, or the spouse is unable to care for the child. It is important to note that this right will not apply if the employee's or his spouse's workplace had adequate arrangements for caring for the child.
According to the law, an employer shall not dismiss an employee for absence from work or failure to perform work during the temporary order period, in order to care for a child who is with him, due to the service of the employee's spouse or the other parent of his child as a soldier. However, the law does not explicitly address the entitlement to paid absence in the event of the closure of educational institutions. Therefore, there is no clear provision in the law that requires the employer to pay wages in such a case. In the case of justified absence, the law states that it will not be considered a break in the continuity of work and will not affect the rights dependent on the employee's seniority.

No, as a general rule, it is prohibited to fire a pregnant woman. According to the Women's Employment Law, an employer may not fire an employee who is pregnant and has not yet entered the period of childbirth and parenthood, except with special permission from the Minister of Labor and Social Affairs. This provision applies to both permanent and temporary employees, provided that the employee has worked for the same employer or in the same workplace for at least six months. The Minister will not authorize dismissals if, in his opinion, they are related to pregnancy.
No, the order does not apply only to a collective agreement. According to Section 27 of the Emergency Labor Service Law, 5727-1967, the provisions in this chapter or issued pursuant to it shall be binding notwithstanding anything stated in any employment contract or collective agreement. In other words, the order applies to both individual employment contracts and collective agreements.

No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (reserve call-up order) without a special permit. According to the law, it is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call-up to reserve service, or his expected service in the reserves. If the employee is fired in violation of these provisions, the firing is void. In addition, there is protection against firing during and after reserve service: 1. For reserve service of more than two consecutive days and less than 60 days: The employee may not be fired during the service and 30 days after its completion without a permit. 2. For reserve service of 60 days or more: The employee may not be fired during the service and 60 days after its completion without a permit. In any case, the employee's absence due to reserve service will not be considered a break in the continuity of his work.
No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (Reserve Order) without special permission. According to the law, it is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or his expected service in reserve service. If the employee serves in the reserves for more than two consecutive days, he may not be fired during his service and for 30 days after the end of his reserve service, except with special permission from the Employment Committee. If the employee serves more than 60 days in the reserves, the period of protection from dismissal is extended to 60 days after the end of his service. In the event of a dismissal in violation of these provisions, the dismissal is void.
Yes, an employee is entitled to payment for absence due to the illness of his minor child, in accordance with the Sick Pay (Absence Due to Child Illness) Law, 5753-1993. The law states that an employee who has a child under the age of 16 is entitled to have days of absence due to the illness of his child credited against his accumulated period of illness. The number of days and conditions depend on the family situation: 1. If the spouse works and is not absent from work - up to 8 days per year. 2. If the child is in the sole custody of the employee or the employee is an independent parent - up to 16 days per year. It is important to note that the law emphasizes that it is intended to add to the employee's rights and not detract from them.

Yes, there is a law that protects the rights of workers during emergencies. The law is called the "Protection of Workers During Emergencies Law, 2006." Section 9 of this law states that the provisions of the law are intended to add to the rights of an employee under any law, collective agreement or employment contract, and not to detract from them. In other words, the law provides additional protection for workers during emergencies beyond their normal rights. It is important to note that according to Section 19 of the law, these provisions also apply to the state as an employer.

According to the law, a public employer is obligated to pay wages to an employee who is determined to be abducted or missing as a result of hostilities or war that began on October 7, 2023. The payment will be at the employee's regular wage, without supplements, up to a ceiling of NIS 1,510 per day. In the case of an employee who is murdered, the law does not specifically address the payment of wages. However, the law prohibits the dismissal of an employee who is abducted or missing, and states that such dismissals will be void. It is important to note that the law refers to the period from the date of the declaration of a special situation in the home front (October 7, 2023) until the end of the declaration period or until the end of significant military operations, whichever is later.
According to the law, a public employer is obliged to protect the rights of employees in the event of abduction or absence as a result of hostilities. The law prohibits the dismissal of an employee who is abducted or absent as a result of hostilities or war, as stipulated by the Compensation for Family Members of Abductees and Missing Persons in Hostilities Law. In the case of an employee who was abducted, missing or murdered on 7.10.2023, the public employer is not permitted to dismiss the employee or terminate his employment. Furthermore, the law also protects family members of the abductee or missing person, and prohibits their dismissal due to absence from work related to this situation. Regarding the payment of wages, the law does not specify specific guidelines for this case. However, the family is advised to contact the public employer and inquire about their specific rights regarding the continued payment of wages and other social conditions.
 Under the law, family members of soldiers who have died or been disabled in war are also entitled to certain protections. The law defines "family members" as including: 1. Spouse 2. Children and stepchildren who were dependent on the soldier at the time of his enlistment 3. Parents who were dependent on the soldier for at least half of their income at the time of his enlistment, or who immigrated to Israel after enlistment These protections apply to family members of soldiers who died during their military service or up to one year after their discharge as a result of illness or injury incurred in service.

According to the law, a temporary employee can be fired. A temporary employee is defined in the Annual Leave Law as an employee who has worked for less than 75 consecutive days for one employer or one workplace. Such an employee does not have the special protections that apply to permanent employees, and therefore can be fired under general labor laws. However, it should be noted that a temporary employee also has certain rights, such as vacation pay, as specified in the law.

According to the Annual Leave Law, the number of days of annual leave depends on the employee's seniority with the employer: - In the first five years: 16 days of leave per year - In the sixth year: 18 days of leave - In the seventh year: 21 days of leave - From the eighth year onwards: an additional day for each year of work, up to a maximum of 28 days of leave per year. It is important to note that this entitlement is for employees who have worked at least 200 days per year. For employees who have worked less than 200 days, the number of days of leave will be calculated proportionally.
According to the Hours of Work and Rest Law, 5711-1951, the payment for overtime is as follows: - For the first two additional hours on the same day: no less than 125% of the regular wage (1.25 times the regular wage). - For each additional hour beyond the first two hours: no less than 150% of the regular wage (1.5 times the regular wage). It is important to note that "regular wage" includes all supplements that the employer pays to the employee.

 According to the law, there is no express prohibition on an employee coming to work during normal hours while on an open 8-day leave. In fact, the law states that “as long as there are no restrictions from the military or the employer, work may be performed concurrently with reserve duty.” Therefore, if there are no specific restrictions from the military or the employer, the employee is permitted to come to work even when on an open 8-day leave. The employer cannot arbitrarily prohibit this just because the employee is on an 8-day leave.
 According to the law, there is no specific reference to preventing participation in professional training during Order 8. However, the law states that as long as there are no restrictions on behalf of the military or the employer, work can be performed concurrently with reserve duty. Therefore, if the professional training is considered part of regular work, and the military has not restricted it, the employer should not prevent the employee from participating in it simply because he is on Order 8. However, if there are other practical reasons besides the fact that the employee is on Order 8, the employer may be entitled to restrict participation.

Yes, if you serve in the reserves on Friday-Saturday (the weekly rest day), you should receive higher pay. According to the Working Hours and Rest Law, an employee who works during the weekly rest day is entitled to payment of at least 150% of his regular salary for those hours. That is, if you serve in the reserves on Friday-Saturday, you are entitled to regular reserve pay plus at least 50% for working during the weekly rest day.

According to the Working Hours and Rest Law, an employee who is employed during the weekly rest hours (Friday-Saturday) is entitled to increased payment. The employer must pay the employee a wage for these hours that will not be less than 150% of his regular wage. In other words, an employee who works Friday-Saturday does receive a wage, and even at a higher rate than the regular wage. In addition, the law stipulates that the employer must give the employee alternative rest hours in place of the weekly rest hours he worked, in the number and time specified in the work permit.
According to the law, the employer is supposed to pay the employee sick pay. Section 2(a) of the Sick Pay Law states that "an employee who is absent from work due to illness shall be entitled to receive from his employer" the sick pay. However, there are cases in which the employer may be exempt from this payment, for example if the employee has insured the employee with sick pay insurance through a provident fund under certain conditions, as detailed in Section 8 of the law.

According to the law, the other parent of your children has a number of rights: 1. Right to parenting time: The other parent may exercise the right to parenting time alone or alternately with his/her partner, subject to certain conditions such as the partner's consent and submitting a declaration to the employer. 2. Absence due to a child's illness: If the child is under 16, the other parent is entitled to up to 8 days of absence per year at the expense of his/her sick days, provided that the partner is not absent from work for this purpose. 3. Rights for an adoptive, intended or foster parent: If applicable, special rights apply to the other parent in these contexts. It is important to note that the exact rights depend on the specific circumstances and the employment status of both parents.
As an employee and student in reserve service, you have a number of important rights: 1. Continuity of work: Your absence from work due to reserve service will not be considered a break in the continuity of your work. 2. Protection against dismissal: The employer may not dismiss you due to reserve service, call-up to service or anticipated service, including due to the frequency or duration of service. 3. Extended protection: If reserve service exceeds two consecutive days, the employer may not dismiss you or reduce the scope of your job or your income without special permission, even for 30 days after the end of reserve service. 4. Preservation of seniority rights: The period of reserve service will be considered a period of work for the purpose of calculating seniority and rights dependent on seniority (such as vacation, illness, severance pay, etc.). 5. Compensation: Compensation for reserve service is provided by the National Insurance Institute. In some cases, if you work for a monthly salary, you may receive your compensation through your employer. It is important to note that these rights apply to you as an employee, regardless of whether you are a student.
According to the law, the spouse of a reserve service member has a number of rights: 1. Protection against dismissal: An employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee due to her spouse's reserve service. 2. Protection against taking unpaid leave: An employer is prohibited from taking an employee on unpaid leave during her spouse's reserve service period or for 14 days after the end of the service, except with a special permit. 3. Entitlement to paid days of absence: The spouse is entitled to a number of paid days of absence, depending on the number of days of her spouse's cumulative reserve service. For example, if the spouse served 31-60 days of reserve service, the spouse is entitled to 2 days of paid absence. 4. Maintaining continuity of work: The spouse's absence from work during her spouse's reserve service period will not be considered a break in the continuity of her work and will not affect the rights dependent on seniority. It is important to note that these rights depend on certain conditions and the length of reserve service, and the specific details must be checked in each case.

 According to the Law for the Protection of Workers in Times of Emergency, the dismissal of an employee during a war due to absence or failure to perform work arising from the security situation is prohibited and void. In the event of such dismissal, the employee is entitled to return to his job. The law does not specify special compensation conditions for an employee dismissed during the war, but rather states that the dismissals themselves are void. However, if the employee is unlawfully dismissed, he may file a claim with the Labor Court within 12 months from the date the cause of action arose. It is important to note that the law also protects contractor employees, and states that the termination of employment, permanent or temporary, of a contractor employee is considered a dismissal for this purpose.

Yes, there are special rights for employees during emergencies under Israeli law. The Workers' Protection During Emergencies Law, 2006, provides certain protections for employees, such as prohibiting dismissals and maintaining continuity of work. Section 9 of the law states that the provisions of the law are intended to add to the employee's rights under any law, collective agreement or employment contract, and not to detract from them. However, it is important to note that there are certain groups of employees who are not included in these protections, such as police officers, prison guards, employees of the General Security Service and the Mossad, and members of rescue organizations. In addition, the law allows for the accumulation of vacation days under certain conditions related to the state of emergency, without the need for the employer's consent.
 No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (reserve recruitment order) without special permission. The law prohibits firing an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or his expected service in the reserves. In the case of reserve service exceeding two consecutive days, it is prohibited to fire the employee during the service and for 30 days after the end of the service, except with special permission from the Employment Committee. If the employee has served more than 60 days in the reserves, the period of protection from firing is extended to 60 days after the end of the service. If firings were carried out in violation of these provisions, the firings are void.

 According to the extension order for reserve servicemen, the spouse of a reserve serviceman is entitled to paid days of absence according to the number of days of her spouse's cumulative reserve service. The entitlement is as follows:- No entitlement up to 30 days of reserve service- 2 days of paid absence for 31-60 days of reserve service- 4 days of paid absence for 61-90 days of reserve service- 6 days of paid absence for 91-120 days of reserve service- 8 days of paid absence for 121 days of reserve service and above. To exercise the entitlement, the spouse must notify the employer of her intention to utilize the days of absence and present confirmation of her spouse's reserve service.
 According to the law, there is no special restriction on an employee's resignation after returning from maternity leave. An employee may resign from her job at any time, even if she is under contract with the employer. However, attention must be paid to the notice requirements set forth in the law or in the employment contract. It is important to note that resignation within 60 days of the end of maternity leave may be considered a legally dismissed resignation, which entitles the employee to severance pay.
According to the law, there is no specific reference to the cancellation of workdays in general. However, the law addresses issues related to weekly rest: 1. An employee may notify the employer, within three days of the request, that he does not agree to work on the weekly rest days for religious reasons. 2. The employer may require the employee to submit a written statement to this effect within seven days. Regarding the cancellation of regular workdays, there is no express reference in the presented law. In the event of additional questions or specific cases, it is recommended to seek professional legal advice.
According to the law, an employee does not have the right to demand that the employer not report to work without a justifiable reason. The law protects employees from dismissal in certain cases of absence from work during a state of emergency, such as: 1. When an official order has been issued during an attack or a special situation on the home front that prevents the employee from reporting to work. 2. When the employee is required to look after his child due to the closure of an educational institution due to the state of emergency. 3. When the employee is required to look after his child due to the spouse's reserve service. If these or similar circumstances defined by law do not exist, the employee is obliged to report to work as usual, unless he has reached a different agreement with the employer.
An employee who has completed reserve service may not be dismissed without permission from the Employment Committee within a certain period after the end of service, depending on the length of reserve service: 1. If the employee has served more than 60 days in the reserve, he may not be dismissed without permission for 60 days after the end of reserve service. 2. If the employee has served more than two consecutive days and less than 60 days, he may not be dismissed without permission for 30 days after the end of reserve service. In addition, dismissals due to reserve service are prohibited, and dismissals will be voided if they were made for this reason.

The employer must allow the employee to express his views before making a final decision in his case. Therefore, dismissals without a hearing may be considered unlawful dismissals. Regarding wage withholding, the law does not directly address this issue in the sections provided. According to the Emergency Workers Protection Law, 2006, an employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee for his absence from work or failure to perform work during the temporary order period due to the existence of one of the following cases: 1. The employee has evacuated his place of residence, which is located in a locality listed in the appendix to the law. 2. The employee is the parent or spouse of someone who has been determined to be abducted or missing as a result of hostilities or war. 3. The employee himself is abducted or missing as a result of hostilities or war. In these cases, if the employee is dismissed in violation of the provisions of the law, the dismissal is void. However, if the employee is absent on his own initiative without the circumstances defined in the law having existed, the responsibility lies with the employee.

According to the Sick Pay Law, an employer is not permitted to dismiss an employee who is absent from work due to illness, during the period of entitlement to sick pay accrued to him by law or collective agreement, up to the maximum period of entitlement. However, there are exceptions to this rule: 1. If the employer gave the employee advance notice of dismissal before the employee was absent due to illness. 2. If the workplace has ceased to operate permanently or the employer has been declared bankrupt. If the employee is dismissed in violation of these provisions, the dismissal is void.

According to the Sick Pay Law, 5736-1976, the answer to the question depends on the type of employee: 1. For a salaried employee - the period of illness includes all days of his illness, including weekly rest days and holidays. That is, Friday and Saturday are considered part of the sick days. 2. For a salaried employee who worked full-time - the period of illness includes all days of his illness, excluding weekly rest days and holidays. In this case, Friday and Saturday are not considered part of the sick days. 3. For a salaried employee who did not work full-time (works intermittently) - the calculation is more complex and depends on the scope of his work in the period preceding the illness. It is important to note that if the employee used to work on weekly rest days and holidays according to a permit, these days will be taken into account in the period of illness.

According to the Working Hours and Rest Law, on a workday of six hours or more, the employer must give the employee a break of at least 45 minutes (3/4 hour) for rest and a meal. Of this time, there must be one continuous break of at least half an hour. It should be noted that on the day before the weekly rest or the day before a holiday, the minimum break is half an hour. It is important to know that this break cannot exceed three hours. Also, if the break lasts half an hour or more, the employee is allowed to leave the workplace, unless his presence is necessary for the work process or the operation of the equipment.

According to Section 10(b) of the Annual Leave Law, the reference to a salaried employee (Section 2) is to an employee who does not receive a salary on a monthly basis or for a longer period. In your case, if you receive a monthly global salary, you are considered an employee whose remuneration for work is paid on a monthly basis, and therefore the relevant calculation for you is according to Section 10(b)(1) - the salary you would have received if you had not taken leave and continued to work.
According to the law, the calculation of sick days depends on the type of employee: 1. For a salaried employee - the sick period includes all of his sick days, including weekly rest days (Friday-Saturday) and holidays. 2. For a salaried employee who works full-time - the sick period includes all of his sick days, excluding weekly rest days and holidays. 3. For a salaried employee who does not work full-time (working intermittently) - the sick period is calculated proportionally, excluding weekly rest days and holidays. Therefore, whether your sick leave includes Friday-Saturday depends on your type of employment - whether you work for a salary or for wages, and in the case of a salaried employee - whether you work full-time or intermittently.

When serving more than 90 days in the reserves, there are several implications: 1. Protection from dismissal: An employee who served more than 60 days in the reserves during the service and for 60 days after its completion cannot be dismissed without special permission. 2. In general, it is permissible to fire employees in Israel. However, the law restricts firings in a number of special cases:1. It is prohibited to fire an employee for absence from work during a state of emergency or war, when an official order has been issued preventing the employee from coming to work.2. It is prohibited to fire an employee who is absent from work to look after his or her child due to the closure of an educational institution during a state of emergency.3. It is prohibited to fire a pregnant employee without special permission from the Ministry of Labor, if she has worked for at least 6 months with the same employer.4. It is prohibited to fire an employee or worker during maternity and parental leave and for 60 days after returning from this leave, without special permission.In all other cases, firings of employees are permitted subject to regular labor laws.

Yes, there are general permits dealing with overtime. According to Section 11 of the Working Hours and Rest Law, the Minister of Labor may permit the employment of an employee overtime in a number of cases, such as: 1. When the needs of supply and essential services require it. 2. In times of emergency when the state of the economy requires it. 3. In non-industrial public services. 4. On guard duty. 5. In places of care for patients, pharmacies, convalescent homes, and institutions for the care of the elderly or children. 6. In restaurants, hotels, cafes, and cultural, sports, and entertainment establishments. 7. In seasonal work or in exceptional cases when there is unusual temporary work pressure. 8. For reasons related to the needs of the economy and the economy or the unique needs of a particular type of work, workplace, or industry. It is important to note that the Minister of Labor may set terms and conditions for these permits, including determining the range of permitted overtime hours.

Under Israeli law, there is no defined maximum limit on the additional pay that an employee receives from his employer. However, it is important to note that employing an employee overtime is prohibited unless it is permitted under certain sections of the law. Therefore, while there is no limit on the amount of additional pay, there are restrictions on the employment of overtime itself.

The Committee will grant a permit only for special reasons and if it is proven that the impairment is not due to reserve duty. Job scope refers to the number of hours worked or the percentage of the employee's job. According to the law, an employer is prohibited from reducing the job scope of an employee whose dismissal is restricted, except with a special permit. Reducing the job scope can be expressed in reducing working hours or reducing the job scope. It should be noted that a temporary change in the job scope at the employee's request due to a medical condition, in accordance with a written medical certificate, is not considered to be a reduction in the job scope under the law.

According to the Sick Pay Law, 1976, in your case: 1. The first day of illness is considered a Sunday, even if you were not supposed to work on it. 2. The second day of illness is Monday, when you were supposed to work. 3. Regarding payment: - For Sunday (the first day of illness) - there is no entitlement to sick pay. - For Monday (the second day of illness) - entitlement to half of the sick pay. - For Thursday (the fifth day of illness) - entitlement to full sick pay. It is important to note that the amount of sick pay will be in accordance with the salary you would have received if you had worked on these days.
According to the law, for an hourly employee who does not work regularly (intermittent worker): 1. The first day of illness is considered Sunday, when you became ill. 2. Regarding payment: - For the first day (Sunday) - no entitlement to sick pay. - For the second and third days (Monday and Tuesday) - entitlement to half of the sick pay. - From the fourth day (Thursday) - entitlement to full sick pay. 3. There is no entitlement to payment for the third and fourth days, because you were not supposed to work on them according to your work schedule. 4. The amount of sick pay will be in accordance with the regular salary you would have received if you had worked on these days. It is important to note that the exact calculation of sick pay for an intermittent employee is based on the ratio between the actual working days and the possible working days in the quarter preceding the illness.
According to the law, a regular workday shall not exceed eight hours of work. However, on Friday (the day before the weekly rest), the workday shall not exceed seven hours of work. In the case described, if Friday is not a regular workday according to the employment contract, and the employee chose to work on this day with the consent of the employer, these hours should be considered overtime. This is because they exceed the regular workdays defined in the employment contract (Sunday to Thursday). It is important to note that employing an employee overtime is prohibited, unless it is permitted under certain sections of the law. Therefore, it is recommended to verify with the employer that working overtime on Friday is approved and meets the requirements of the law.
 According to the Sick Pay Law, 1976, the amount of sick pay is determined by the wages the employee would have been entitled to receive if he had continued working. In the case of an employee whose work hours vary daily as needed, the law does not provide specific guidance. However, it can be concluded that the regular wage should be calculated based on the average hours and wages in the period prior to the illness. For additional details or clarifications, it is recommended to seek professional legal advice.

According to the law, in the event of dismissal, an employee may be entitled to compensation. The amount of compensation and the date of its payment depend on the specific circumstances of the dismissal and the decision of the Employment Committee. In the event of a violation of the employee's rights, the Employment Committee may award compensation in an amount equal to five times the employee's monthly wage, or five times the average wage in the economy (whichever is higher). However, the Committee may determine a different amount for special reasons. Regarding the date of payment, the law states that the Employment Committee will determine the date for payment of the compensation in an order it issues. If the compensation is not paid on the date set, linkage differences and interest will be added to them. It is important to note that each case will be examined on its own merits, and the exact compensation and date of payment will be determined by the Employment Committee in accordance with the specific circumstances of the case.

The right to accrue vacation days is related to dismissals in the following way: According to the Annual Leave Law, when an employee ceases his employment (including in the case of dismissal) before he has been granted the vacation he is entitled to, the employer must pay him vacation pay. This amount is equal to the vacation pay that would have been payable to the employee if he had gone on vacation on the day he stopped working. In other words, even if the employee did not use the vacation days he accumulated during his employment, he is entitled to receive payment for them upon termination of employment, including in the case of dismissal.
According to the law, even if an employee is fired before the end of a full year of work, he is still entitled to the redemption of the vacation days he has accumulated. Section 13 of the Annual Leave Law states that if an employee stops working before he has been granted the vacation he is entitled to, the employer must pay him vacation redemption in an amount equal to the vacation pay that would have been payable to him if he had gone on vacation on the day he stopped working. In other words, even if the employee is fired before the end of the year, he is entitled to receive payment for the vacation days he has accumulated up to the date of dismissal. It is important to note that the law protects the rights of employees and prohibits dismissals that are intended to prevent the employee from receiving rights. If the employee believes that he was fired with the aim of preventing him from receiving rights, he may apply to the appropriate legal authorities.

 Under Israeli law, there is no specific reference to additional pay in the event of mental injury. According to Israeli law, there is no explicit prohibition on dismissing an employee after 11 and a half months of work. However, it is important to note the employee's rights upon termination of employment: 1. Vacation pay: According to Section 13 of the Annual Leave Law, the employer must pay the employee vacation pay for vacation days that have been accrued and not used by the date of termination of employment. 2. Advance notice: The employee must be given advance notice of his dismissal in accordance with the law. 3. Severance pay: An employee who has worked for less than a year is not entitled to severance pay by law, unless otherwise provided in the employment contract or collective agreement. 4. Payment of final salary: The employee must be paid his final salary, including all rights due to him up to the date of dismissal. It is important to note that it is necessary to check whether there are any special circumstances (such as pregnancy or reserve service) that may affect the possibility of dismissal.
According to Israeli law, there is no explicit prohibition on dismissing an employee after 13 months of work, as long as the dismissal is made in accordance with the law and existing agreements. However, there are a number of conditions that an employee is entitled to upon dismissal: 1. Vacation pay: According to Section 13 of the Annual Leave Law, the employer must pay the employee vacation pay for the vacation days that he has accumulated and not used by the date of termination of employment. 2. Accumulation of vacation days: According to Section 7 of the Annual Leave Law, the employee may, with the employer's consent, accumulate vacation days beyond 7 days per year, and carry the balance over to the next two years of work. It is important to note that there may be additional conditions in accordance with collective agreements or the employee's personal employment contract.

According to the Women's Labor Law, a pregnant employee is permitted to be absent from work during the months of pregnancy if a doctor has certified in writing that her medical condition requires it. This absence is considered an absence due to illness. According to the law, wives of reservists are entitled to a number of rights: 1. Protection against dismissal: An employer is prohibited from dismissing an employee due to her spouse's reserve service, or due to his call-up for reserve service or anticipated service. 2. Protection against taking unpaid leave: If an employee has a child, the employer is prohibited from taking her on unpaid leave during her spouse's reserve service period or for 14 days after the end of the service, except with a special permit. 3. Paid days of absence: Spouses of reservists are entitled to paid days of absence according to the number of days of cumulative reserve service of the spouse. For example, for 31-60 days of reserve service, they are entitled to 2 days of paid absence, and up to 8 days for 121 days of reserve service or more. 4. Maintaining continuity of work: Absence from work during the spouse's reserve service period will not be considered a break in continuity of work and will not affect rights dependent on seniority.

Without this permit, the employer is not permitted to fire a pregnant employee who meets this seniority requirement.

Protection against dismissal: An employer is prohibited from firing an employee due to the reserve service of their spouse or the other parent of their child. 2. Shortening the workday: An employee is entitled to shorten the workday by one hour when the spouse is in the reserve, under certain conditions (such as service of at least 5 consecutive days and a child up to the age of 13). 3. Protection against taking unpaid leave: In certain cases, an employer is prohibited from taking an employee on unpaid leave during their spouse's reserve service. 5. Maintaining continuity of rights: An employee's absence during their spouse's reserve service will not affect the continuity of their employment or the rights dependent on seniority.

Yes, a Muslim, Druze or Christian employee may choose a day of rest other than Shabbat. According to the Hours of Work and Rest Law, 5711-1951, Section 7(b)(2), for a non-Jew, the weekly rest will include the Sabbath or the first or sixth day of the week, all according to what is acceptable to him as his weekly rest day. That is, a non-Jewish employee may choose his weekly rest day according to what is acceptable to him.

However, the employer must consider the employee's request to work remotely in good faith and in a matter-of-fact manner given the existing circumstances. According to the law, the right to be absent for one hour a day when the spouse is in the reserves applies only to full-time employees. The law does not explicitly address part-time employees. Therefore, a part-time employee is not automatically entitled to be absent for one hour a day when his or her spouse is in the reserves. However, it is recommended to check with the employer whether there is an internal policy or collective agreement that allows flexibility in this regard for part-time employees as well.

According to the law, the duty to hear is granted to every employee before his dismissal or before a material change in the terms of his employment. The employer must allow the employee to express his views before making a final decision in his case. In this case, the employee received a summons to a hearing and submitted written arguments. Submitting written arguments can be considered fulfilling the duty to hear, but this depends on the specific circumstances of the case and the manner in which the process was conducted. It is important to note that the hearing is intended to allow the employee to present his position fully, and therefore it is necessary to examine whether the written arguments indeed fully satisfy the purpose of the hearing.

 Yes, it is possible to work and perform reserve service at the same time, subject to a number of conditions: 1. There are no restrictions from the military on working concurrently with reserve service. 2. There are no restrictions from the employer on working concurrently with reserve service. In the case of remote work (working from home) during reserve service, the employer's consent is also required. It is important to note that if there are no such restrictions, there is an obligation to report for work concurrently with reserve service.
According to the law, there are several criteria for approving a change in an employee's working conditions: 1. Reasonableness of the change: According to Section 9e of the Working Hours and Rest Law, an employer may reasonably change the working conditions and work procedures of an employee who has notified that he does not agree to work on the weekly rest days. 2. Collective agreement: According to Section 5 of the law, the Minister of Labor may approve changes in working hours within the framework of a collective agreement, provided that the circumstances justify this and that the average working hours do not exceed certain limits. 3. Obligation to hear: Before a material change in the terms of employment, the employer must allow the employee to be heard before making a final decision. It should be noted that certain changes may require the employee's express consent, such as in the case of negative vacation balance.

 The duty to hold a hearing is imposed on every employee before his dismissal or before a material change in the terms of his employment. This means that the employer must allow the employee to express his views before making a final decision in his case. It is important to note that there is no prohibition in law on holding a hearing during a period of illness, unless the court determines otherwise.
 According to the law, the right to a shortened workday when a spouse is in the reserves applies only to full-time employees. The law specifically states that one of the conditions for eligibility is that the employee is employed full-time according to the practice at the workplace. Therefore, part-time employees are not entitled to a shortened workday when their spouse is serving in the reserves.
 The law does not specifically address changes to the holiday calendar. However, the law does list holidays and observances for various religious and ethnic groups in Israel. Changes to the holiday calendar may affect employees’ days of rest, but there are no explicit instructions on how to make such changes. If you have specific questions about changes to the holiday calendar in the workplace, it is recommended to consult with your human resources department or a qualified legal professional.
According to the law, the Minister of Labor may establish by regulations, for certain jobs, a weekly rest period that will be less than 36 hours, but not less than 25 consecutive hours. In addition, an employer may reasonably change the working conditions and work procedures of an employee who has notified that he does not agree to work on the weekly rest days. It is important to note that these changes must be reasonable and in accordance with the law.

However, the law does not specifically address changing the employee’s holiday schedule. In this case, the agreement between the employer and the employee and the existing procedures in the workplace must be examined. If there is no agreement or specific instructions on the subject, negotiations between the parties or consultation with a professional in the field of labor law may be required.

 Under Israeli law, the decision to work remotely is part of the employer’s administrative right. According to the law, you have several rights to be absent from work as the wife of a reserve soldier: 1. You are entitled to be absent from work for one hour a day, starting from the first day of your spouse's reserve service, provided that: - Your spouse's reserve period is at least 5 consecutive days - You have a child under the age of 13 who is with you - You are employed full-time - You have notified your employer of the exercise of the right and present confirmation of your spouse's reserve service 2. In addition, according to the extension order for reserve service members, you are entitled to paid days of absence depending on the number of days of your spouse's cumulative reserve service: - 31-60 reserve days: 2 days of paid absence - 61-90 reserve days: 4 days of paid absence - 91-120 reserve days: 6 days of paid absence - 121 reserve days and above: 8 days of paid absence It is important to note that these rights are in addition to your regular absence rights according to law.

 No, it is prohibited to fire employees who have received Order 8 (Reserve Recruitment Order) without special permission. According to the law, it is prohibited to fire an employee due to his service in the reserves, his call to reserve service, or his expected service in the reserves. If the employee serves in the reserves for more than two consecutive days, he may not be fired during the period of service and for 30 days after the end of the service, without permission from the Employment Committee. If the duration of service exceeds 60 days, the period of protection from dismissal is extended to 60 days after the end of the service. Dismissals made in violation of these provisions will be void.

 According to the law, if you did not work on Fridays, Saturdays, and holidays, and yet received a regular salary as if you had worked, you are not entitled to additional compensation for the missing days. The law states that an employee who was employed during the weekly rest hours is entitled to payment of at least 150% of his regular salary for those hours. However, if you did not actually work on these days and already received regular salary for them, there is no legal obligation to pay you an additional supplement or additional compensation. It is important to note that if this is a special arrangement or collective agreement at your workplace, there may be different provisions. In such a case, it is recommended to check your specific terms of employment with your employer or the collective agreement that applies to you.

 According to the law, there is no specific reference to the case where the employee requests to terminate his employment due to a medical condition. However, the law refers to situations where the employee has temporary medical limitations. In such a case, the employer may offer the employee other suitable work, full or part-time, at the same workplace. If the employee refuses the proposed alternative work, he may lose his entitlement to sick pay. It is important to note that if the employer does not offer suitable alternative work, the employee is entitled to sick pay as usual. It is recommended that the employee discuss with the employer the options available to him according to his medical condition and working conditions.

According to the law, the Minister of Defense has a number of powers regarding labor issues: 1. The Minister of Defense is responsible for implementing the Discharged Soldiers Law (Return to Work). 2. The Minister of Defense may delegate powers to the Supervisor of Employment of Discharged Soldiers regarding the implementation of the law. 3. The Minister of Defense may, with the consent of the Minister of Labor and Social Insurance and after consultation with the Center of General Labor Bureaus, issue regulations on issues such as priority for employment for family members of soldiers and discharged soldiers. 4. In IDF or defense establishments, the consent of someone appointed by the Minister of Defense for this purpose is required for the use of certain powers. 5. The Minister of Defense may delegate his powers to another person under certain sections of the Emergency Labor Service Law.

 According to the Hours of Work and Rest Law, 5711-1951, employing an employee overtime is prohibited, if not permitted under Section 10, or if not permitted under Section 11. Also, a work week may not exceed forty-five hours of work. Regarding work over 12 hours a day, the law does not specifically address this in the sections presented.
